Thus begins the chapter of the third part: of restoration. This chapter is very necessary in the art of medicine; and it is about the restoration of fractures and dislocations that occur in the bones.
Oh, son, know that these foolish doctors and the vulgar boast about this chapter and those who have never looked at the books of the Ancients nor read even one letter from them.
And for this reason, it is made that this manner of the learned in our land is deprived; and you must not forget this at any time; and I have not adopted this, but by length of reading the books of the Ancients and study on the understanding of those, until I extracted the science from them.
And I have directed myself to experiment and study in the length of my life.
And I have already described in this chapter all that my science contains and on which my best experience has been passed; because I have now abridged it for you others and delivered it from the occupation of elongation; and I have abridged it at the end of abridgement; and I have shown it at the end of declaration; and I have formed many forms of the instruments that are administered in it, as it has been done by addition of declaration, as I have done in two previous chapters.
First Chapter.
Summaries and collections from the essence of bone fractures, from which a preface is necessary before we begin the remembrance of broken and diverse members of each one. Therefore, I say that in the prologue of this chapter, the summaries of the sermon; and the chapters to the understanding of which at the beginning, the artificers are forced and stand upon the truth of those; so it is for you and those who are prompt to know or learn this noble art from others than you.
And therefore I say that when you encounter a fracture, or dislocation, or sprain, or wound, then you must, at the beginning, apply yourself to phlebotomy or to leavening that, or one of the two, if no cause forbids it, as the weakness of virtue, or the one to whom you encounter is a child, or old in decrepitude, or the time of vehement heat, or of vehement cold.
And then be content in his food of fresh herbs and of bird meats and of crabs; and forbid thick meats and to fill him with foods and of all food that fills the veins with blood, until you are sure of a healthy cautery, and if you do not fear effusion of matter to the limb.
And then return the patient to his first regimen to which he is accustomed.
And therefore, as the broken bone begins to be restored, then the patient must be fed nutritious foods of nourishment, thick and heavy, in which there is viscosity, such as foods made of boiled wheat with water, and rice, and capons, and fish, and cow bellies, and eggs, and fresh poultry, and thick meat and similar to those, and with this regimen is made coagulation of fracture more easily and better.
And know that the broken bones when they are in the young men who are in the first youth and in the decrepit, it is not possible that they be continued and consolidated according to their nature by the drying of their members.
However, nature adjusts and binds over the broken bone from every part any cause seeming to glut in which there is thickness that adheres and strengthens it until it is at the same time conglutinated and glued together, until they come to the end of fortitude and firmness, as fire at the beginning, until no cause forbids it from its works.
And for this reason it is necessary that you pause the foods of the patient, foods in which there is heaviness and viscosity and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the species of fracture are diverse according to the diversity of the limbs, for the fracture of the bone of the leg is diverse from the fracture of the bone of the head; and the fracture of the bone of the foot is diverse from the fracture of the bone of the hand; and similarly all the other members are diverse among themselves.
And I come with remembrance of all species of fracture, explained in their distinct chapter from the other.
Many varieties of fracture in themselves. And they are diversified the species of fracture of the bone in themselves; for there is an arched fracture, another that happens in stars; and sometimes it is made a fracture according to the length of the bone: and there are fractures of the saga, and stars, and additions proceeding and non-proceeding, as it is done in the pen when it is prepared to write, and it is a fracture with plaque and disruption in the head, and it is a small fissure fracture.
And to every species is engendered or torment proper in its restoration according to what will come remembrance of it distinct in its places.
Many signs of fracture of the bone. And by this is known the fracture of the bone: it is the twisting of it, and from that prominence and appearance of it to the touch, and so of it when you compress it with your hand.
And therefore when there is no apparent twisting in the place, nor is it felt when you touch the bone with agitation and the patient does not feel great pain, then there is no fracture here.
It is possible that there is a slight twisting or fissure, in which case you must feel it with your hand and with compression of the whole; you must then place on it of the medicines of which remembrance is made at this moment, the one that is suitable for the place; and then be strict with a subtle bandage.
And know that the place when it is arched and not split in two or otherwise that it does not happen in it, as it is remembered the whole part of its comparison, then you must begin from your hour to the rectification of it, having to happen to it a cautery abscess.
And if it happens to it a cautery abscess, then leave it for some days until the abscess ceases; and then bandage it in whichever manner that you can of lightness and ingenuity.
And know that the restoration of it and the healing of it is lighter than of the bone in which the accident happened.
And then strengthen it according to what will come the remembrance of it.
But if in the bone there are wounds, then it is necessary to extend this broken limb from two parts, if it is the arm or the leg; or with your hand if the limb is small; or with two cords or with one cord and the hand.
And be the position with which you place the limb, it is bandaged according to its natural figure, while the parts are extended of the broken limb, then you must perforate that you do make return those additions with ingenuity and in whichever manner that you can in their places.
And guard yourself according to your strength that there does not happen to the patient by your operation pain nor injury; and perforate yourself according to your power that you apply one bone with the other its companion according to their best forms.
And you must in that hour profitably touch with your hand that, and if you see there any other cause, rectify that and bandage it according to the quantity of your virtue.
And avoid vehement extension and compression, as such as foolishly do some physicians who, in their operations, make abscesses happen by their action, or cause contraction in the limb as we have seen happen by the operation of their foolish acts.
And the quality of the constriction of the tranquil limb is that which I recommend; for know that broken limbs are diversified in their smallness and in their greatness and in the form of those.
And of those that are small as the arm and the hand and the foot and similar to those, then you must the envelopment be of very fine cloth.
But those that are gross as the thigh or the leg, then you must in those be envelopment wide and hard, for the wide bandage connects the great limb and strengthens it with equal constriction, so that in it no spot enters.
And then, beginning, your evacuation of equation, pause ligament convenient to this, over the place of that fracture with three or four envelopments according to that the limb merits; and strengthen your hand a little with the ligament.

And then take another bandage and envelop that other time over the broken place with envelopment, and then see in the envelopments to the part further from the fracture; and be your operation in the constriction of the envelopment and in the mollification of that according to what we have said in the first envelopment above.
And then pause between the envelopments of wool or cloth with which is equalized the twisting of the fracture, if in it is twisting; and if not, do not pause in it any cause.

And so that the threads are medium in thickness and subtlety, and they are of linen; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen in the operation of some fools putting their threads of ropes of the flax, and it is a great error, when the bandage is outside equality.
And excessively fine threads are not convenient, because you will not be able to make a bandage with those that you want, and there should be no less than a finger's width between splint and splint.
And if the patient is said to be feeling pain at the extremities of the splints near the bandage in those places, then place some cotton wool or carded wool between those and the patient so that it does not cause injury.
Sometimes a fracture is accompanied by a plague, and accidents disrupt the bone, and I will come to the remedy in that in the chapter according to singularity.
And know that you should not immediately bandage all broken limbs with splints on the first day; the reason for this is that the limb, when it is large, should not be laid upon splints but rather after about five or seven days, or more, according to your certainty of the advent of an abscess.
And this is the manner of splints with which the Ancients restored, which were placed upon fractures and dislocations and twists.

Chapter Second: Of fracture when it happens in the head.
The species of fractures are many, and their figures are diverse, and the causes are distinct, for of fractures some is made by percussion of sword, and the percussion stops all the bone into the skull, which is left the bone, so as it does with the shaft, and for that reason this species is named ascial; or the sword incides some part of the bone, and incides the flesh of it and does not pass the incision into the back of it; and this species of fracture is named levitation absolute.
And the plague of these two fractures or is great or little, and of fracture that is crushing or attrition; and cause of it is percussion with a stone or a fall on stone, and their similar.
And this fracture or is penetrating that already approaches to the pannicle which is left the bone; or is in the face of the bone, and the plague of these two fractures or is great or little.
And of fracture is that which is meguated in the bone, in the subtlety of the hair small, and for that reason this species is named fracture capillar.
And is fracture that is made or by fall or by percussion of stone and its similar, that makes enter some lamina of the bone to the parts within, and is made in the place concavity so as it happens to the payroles of ram when it happens to their percussion and is part of their entrance to the parts within.
And mostly this one is made in the bodies of bones humid, so as it is heads of infants.
And to all these species fractures satagias, so are their pieces as stars separated. And I come with remembrance of all that in its place.
And we know all these species of fracture by detection on those and by inquiry of them with touch and examination of corrupted flesh that is over them.
And especially the capillary is known by this that it is discovered over the bones, and it is cleaned, and liniment is applied to it, because the fracture appears black.
In the cure of a fracture, at the beginning consider the accidents of the patient, and if you see by the accidents that signify by manifest signification over pain, such as vomiting of bile, and extension, and loss of understanding, and abscess of voice, and syncope, and acute fever, and swelling of the joints and redness of those, and similar to those accidents in the major dispositions, it should not be cured.
And if you see non-current accidents, you hope for health, then you begin the cure of it.
The reason is that if it has come to you the afflicted at the beginning as it is afflicted and this is in the days of winter, then you must study in the state of the bone before the fourteenth day according to all disposition.
And if it is in the days of summer, then you must take care of the bone before the seventh day, because that which is left of the bone be corrupted by the panniculus, and that the accidents that we have recounted happen.
And if the fracture has already reached the panniculus covering the brain, and it is with small parts and attrition, then you must introduce the fractured part and rub it, according to that I will recount to you.
And it is that you raise the head of the afflicted patient, and uncover over the bone according to custom and manner to your possible according to the figure of the plague, and that it be light on the patient.
And if it happens to you to detect the bone, flows of blood or pus, then you oppose it with what you have, and it is that you fill the place with cloths infused in wine and olive oil, and make the patient rest until the abscess is settled, and if you are sure of the flows of blood; and then you begin in the circulation of the bone and in its ablation.
And this is according to one of two ways of operation.
And the one way is that you introduce the bone with a straight incisor, and this is the form of it: And then administer another incisor this, a little more wide than that; and this is the form of it:
And then administer more the incisor wider than the second.
And it is necessary that you have a number of various incisors, of which some are wider than others; and some of them are shorter than others and are at the end of the sharpness of their extremities, and are of Indian iron or steel.
And use them with ease in percussion on the incisor so that it does not strike the head and damage it.
And if the bone is strong, hard, then you must perforate in its circuit before you administer the incisors with the drills, which are called non-profund drills.
And they are not called that way unless because those do not stop the term of the bone of the test to that which is near it, because that the drill has a round extremity or margin on that which is left the head of it acute resembling a margin or a small circle, which prohibits being submerged and passing the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take of these drills a large number of which as many as convenient to the thickness of each bone, so that it be to you present in all tests of the skull the extremity of which be acute, the quantity in length and brevity be according to the quantity of thickness of this test.
And this is the form of three species of drills: The quality of perforating in the circuit of the broken bone is that you pause the drill on the bone and revolve that with your fingers until you know that the bone is already penetrated; and then change the drill to another place, and pause that which is between all trace the quantity of the thickness of the ray or environ that.
And then introduce with incisors that which is between each tract of the bone, and do that as subtle as you can on that in the end of lightness, as we have said, until you eradicate the bone, or with your hand, or with some other cause for some instruments which you will have prepared for that, as such as pincers and subtle forceps.
And it is necessary that you guard with all caution that the incisor does not touch any cause of the cephalic.
And therefore, as you will have circuited the bone, and if it is continuous with it, and you will have extracted that, it is necessary that you raise and smooth that in the roughness of it of that which is in the bone with other instrument resembling an incisor, but however it must be more thin and more subtle than the others incisors.
And if there remain any pieces of the small bones and fragments, then take that with ease with that which you owe you of the instruments; and then care for the wound with lichinis and ointments the ones which we have said.
And the other way of operation is lighter, distant from pain, which says Gualia and the lauzec for great lausor; and this is the sermon of it: necessary is to you at the beginning that you begin to discover the part of the bone of the place in which is the fracture most vehement and most manifest, until when you will have discovered that part and you will have placed below it the extremity of that incisor lent and happy of which this is the form:
And the lenticular incision is long and does not cut any cause, and the acute incision is from it in two parts forward in length, thus sometimes; that the lenticular incision be adjusted to the skull with the face of it incident acute in the bone.
And then strike upon the incision with a small mallet until you cut all the bone with ease as it is turned, and if you are sure of the panniculus that no accident happens in it in any manner, and if the physician were the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them; certainly, and if he were sleeping.
Now if any cause annexed to the bone of the panniculus in some places of the bone, cut that of it with the extremity of the lenticular incision, and deliver that of it with ease, because it is delivered of it its nuisance and its pain.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the panniculus, and that which is cut off or is stayed from the bone is the face of it, and remains in it roughness and subtle satagias, then when you have scraped that roughness and eradicated those satagias with subtle razors by which you have prepared the number, of diverse quantities so that it be to you possible to administer of those in every place the most convenient of them and the most right of them according to what in which you are losing your operation that broken bone and its figure.
And then when you administer in your ratio with which razors you cut the bone at the beginning the widest of those razors, and then use it near the most subtle of it, and do not cease doing so according to continuity until you arrive at administering the most tenuous and the most subtle of all those.
But in each of the other fractures, small capillaries and subtle fractures, then when you administer care according to what you are losing to the rectification of it.
And it is cause that it is not guarded over all those who have studied in that art. And stick upon what we have written have shown in great fractures.
And if the panniculus is cut when you eradicate the bone, then when you take cloth of it according to the quantity of the wound, and submerge that in rose oil, and place that upon the orifice of the wound; and then take another cloth folded or tripled and submerge it in wine and rose oil and place that upon the first cloth and do that as lightly as you can upon that, that you do not weigh the skull; and then administer upon that bandage lat, and do not tighten that but for the quantity that the wound has so alone.
And leave that with this care for a day or for two until you are sure of the abscess caut; and then untie that and then administer medicines with which is superfluous dryness, thus as roots of flax and flour of errs, and powder of incense and of aristolochia, and of resemblances to those.
And it is that you make of these medicines powder that be pulverized upon the wound, thus as they are dried; and universally, administer in that all medicine of property of which it is that you abstain and do not bite.
And guard yourself according to your virtue that the wound in your cure be clean; and not be in it dirt of ointment nor other; nor do you leave to add virulence in it; because when it is adjusted upon the panniculus of the brain, it corrupts that and you can that, and it happens upon the patient that evil malicious evil.
And it happens in the skull of the head, as from it is modified the bone and most when it is less cared for, and it is blackness on the surface of it; and therefore consider then if it happens to the patient the accidents that we have said, know that it is pernicious its whole dopte.
And if it does not happen blackness but from the medicine that is placed upon it, and it is in virtue of that medicine that makes that blackness, then when you take of honey one part and of rose oil parts iii., and in those lines a cloth, and then place that upon the skull; and then cure that with the species of cure that convenes until it be healed.
Chapter III.
Of the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that it is not broken of the nose but two parts together most sovereign, or one of those sometimes, because in it you have two bones, because the part left is cartilaginous, and it is not broken and it does not happen to it but attrition and tortuosity and sinuosity.
But if it is broken one of the two parts of it, then when you introduce the said small into its nose, and that you equal that fracture from within with your said finger, and with the said finger outside, until the nose returns according to its natural figure. And be done that by you with ease, and guard yourself that no pain happens to the patient from that your operation.
And if the fracture is in the most sovereign part of the nose, and the said one does not reach to that, then when it be equal with the extremity of the ray in which it be little thickness.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you do the semblance of that.
And guard yourself with the restoration of it in the first day of the fracture, if it is possible; and if not, then about the vii or x you owe the repose of the abscess caut.
And then introduce into the extremity of the nose a piece of cloth of it, if the fracture is in one part, or put two splints, if the fracture is in two parts, and be in the splint thickness of so much quantity that it fills the trough of the nose.
And some of the old restorers say that the poultices should be infused in water, and changed every day, but I have not seen it; rather, I think that the wound should be covered with a bandage of cooked egg white with powdered wool; and then leave it until the bone is set and the cartilage is healed.
And sometimes it is placed inside the nose, in place of a poultice, a piece of cloth soaked in the pus that is enclosed in a lion's skin; and it is used when the nasal fracture is very severe and the patient is not prohibited from breathing.
And this cause is not necessary for you; and if you want, do this, and if you want, do poultices.
And if another time an abscess occurs, cover the nose with a plaster of cerot or cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil, or with diachylon.
But if an abscess does not occur, then you should plaster it outside with a mixture of sesame flour and powdered quince seeds with egg white; and then place a lion's skin over it, and do not touch the nose for any reason.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or are crushed, then you should cut over them, and extract them with instruments that are suitable for them; and then close the incision, and treat it with water that mends and consolidates, and with appropriate plasters.
And if a plague occurs in the nose, then you should treat it with poultices and administer lead pipes until it is completely healed.
Chapter IV.
Of the restoration of the jaw when it is broken.
When the jaw is broken and the fracture of it is not accompanied by a plague, then guard, in this case, if the fracture is only outward, and not broken in two and curved at the parts inside, then its recognition is lighter.
And therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, then you introduce your left index finger into the mouth of the patient; and similarly if the fracture is on the left mandible, then introduce the index finger of your right hand; and push with that the concavity of the fracture inside with ease to the outward parts, with your other hand from the outside of the bone; and in this way gently equalize them.
And if the fracture of the jaw is already separated in two, then you should administer extension of two parts according to rectitude until it is possible the equation of it.
And if there occurs a commotion of teeth to disruption, then strengthen those of which you hope will remain with a thread of gold or silver or lead; and then place over the broken jaw cerot, and then place over it a doubled cloth, and place over the cloth a large splint made decently, or a piece of sole equal to the length of the jaw; and then tie that above according to how the ligation of it is prepared for you and how the application is convenient, until it is not destroyed.
And command the patient to use rest and tranquility, and place their food in lion's broth.
And if you estimate that the figure has already been altered in any way, then you take it off and untie it on the third day; and then rectify what has already been altered from it and plaster it with powdered wool, egg white, or sesame flour, because you will have removed the cerot from it, and place over the plaster lion's wool.
And thus, as it stands attached and adjusted that plaster on it, and the disposition of the bone is not altered, leave it, that you do not untie it until it is healed and strengthens the fracture; because many times this fracture is straightened in three weeks.
And if another time an abscess occurs, then administer what we have said many times in the sedation of it until it is healed, and that abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is with a plague, then consider if it is a piece of bone separated or splinters, then you must subtilize that you remove those splinters with what is convenient for it in the removal of them from the instruments.
And if the orifice of the plague is narrow, then you enlarge it with the fleubotomist according to the quantity of your need.
And then when you will have removed those splinters, and of those nothing is removed again, then you close the orifice of the plague if it is wide; and if not, place over it one of the plasters that are convenient for it and embalm it, until it is healed.
Chapter V.
Of the restoration of the clavicle when it is broken.
Many times the clavicle is broken at the part in front of the muscle, and that fracture is made in one of three ways.
Because it is broken, or separated, or is separated in two parts, the other that does not occur splinters; and it is easy to the restoration of it; or it is so that it occurs splinters in it, and it is difficult to the restoration of it; or it is so that the fracture is with a plague.
And the operation in that, when the fracture is with a plague, is that you make two ministers be present, so that one has the adjutori and follows the broken clavicle, and the other extends the neck with that part.
And then envelop the fracture with your fingers until the form of it is made according to what is convenient, and there is no eminence or concavity in it.
And if you need more extension, then place a pad under the patient's leg with cloth or wool, and its size according to your need; and extend and elevate the shoulder and constrict the pad with your hand until you align the fracture as you wish.
And if you cannot pull the extremity of the shoulder to the outside parts, because it has reached the armpit, then place the patient lying on their neck, and place a cushion of medium size on the muscle of their shoulder.
And the minister presses the muscle of it to the lower parts until the bone of the shoulder is elevated that is in the armpit, and then you rectify the fracture and align it with your hands.
But if you feel splinters of the shoulder and they are bad, then place those on it and extract those splinters with ease.
And if it is held in the bone, then you make an incision of it with one of the scissors that you have prepared for this, because you had placed under the shoulder the instrument that serves the chisels; and it is an instrument of wood or iron of which this is the form:
And it is similar to a spoon of which this is the concavity; and its width according to what you have need of it, according to the size of the bone or the smallness of it.
But the length of it be according to what is possible to you in the operation; that it have two extremities as well, and one extremity be wide and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the plaque which you have incised when you have extracted the splinter from the bone is wide, and there is certainty of pus cauter, then you adjust the two lips of the plaque with suture.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear pus cauter, then you fill the plaque with cloths and powders according to the quantity of the fissure of the plaque.
And if pus cauter occurs, then put a cloth soaked in vinegar and olive oil and wine, and place it over it.
And all, that the wood be not harmful over the broken bone; and for so that you see the patient every day, that when the ligament is softened or you see the wood already be remauguda, then you rectify that and strengthen the binding.
And then place the sleep of the patient on their neck, and place under the leg of it during their sleep at night a small cushion; so that with that be elevated the support of it from the side of it, and therefore is elevated the fracture of the shoulder to elevation of the muscle; or be the arm of it tied to the neck of it.
And do not untie the binding unless you see in the place pus or an abscess, within twelve days.
And then renew the plaster if you have seen the face of it to that, and adjust in the stricture and restore that and leave it until it is restored and coagulated the fracture of the shoulder.
And it is strengthened and comforted according to the month in twenty-eight days, and sometimes in some men it is done in less time than that.
Chapter VI.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the shoulder.
The spatula tart is broken in the side of it and not broken of it but the edges.
And therefore when it is broken the place of it — and it is broken in the middle place of it — and it is not known but by touch, then according to what is the figure of the fracture forces you the preparation of it and reduce that according to the natural figure in every manner of ingenuity which is to you possible.
And then place over the place powder of wheat with egg white and linen cloth; and then place powder of cloth duplicated; and then place over it a piece of thin wood according to the quantity of the spatula all or maybe a little.
But if below the wood is concavity in some of the places of the spatula, then you fill that concavity with linen cloth, and then put the wood according to equality.
And then strengthen above with felt long with constriction decent and with firmness of the wood until it not be remauguda from its place.
And consider the binding every day; and every time that it is softened, tie another time and strengthen it and equal the wood if it is remauguda from its place.
And lay the patient on their side.
And the spatula is restored in 20 days or in 25.
And therefore when this number is complete, then you dissolve the ligament, and that you know for sure that that is of the bones of which the beginning is not feared nor their dissolution.
And if you proceed from the location of the bone of the foot and place the bandage below, then you cut across it, and remove it, and do as we have said in the fracture of the clavicle of the saddle, cauterize if abscess occurs here.
Chapter VII.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the foot.
The foot sometimes is broken in the middle part of it and that is to be treated; but the extremities of it must be treated that they are cicatrized and comminuted.
And of the accidents of it when the middle of it is broken, it is that it declines to the parts of it and occurs to it great pain, and difficulty in the hale, and cough, and fort leu and vomiting of blood; and it is declared in it cavity in the broken bone and is not occult to the feeling.
And the restoration of it is that it be supine the patient on their back, and place between the spaces of it a cushion; and then press to the muscles of it, and adjust the bones of it with the hands of two sides, and subtilia be in the operation of it and in the equation according to every manner that is ad this possible and that convenes ad this, until that returns the figure of the bone according to that cove.
And then place on it plaster and lint and place above the table a subtle of gauze or of caligi and of their likenesses in levitat, because that you envelopes that in cloth; and then subtilia be in lian that on the broken bone that it not be maugut; and go am liadure according to retonditat to the dors some times, and strengthen that am strictura decent; and then consider the liadura in all hour, and when it is mollified strengthen that.
And if necessity provokes the desliament of that, you must corrossio or pruziment, the pain, or the abscess that occurs in the location, then you cut and untie that and eradicate the plaster and rectify that which occurs by that with manner of cure of it; and then reduce the plaster, if times to that the face; and adjust to it stricture until that it be sanat.
Chapter VIII.
Of the restoration of the back when it is fractured.
Know that the fracture of the back does not catch but in places gross that follow the back; and of the extremities of them of the part front, it does not occur to those contricio but for that they are cartilaginous. And cognition of it is not amaguada to the feeling, it must the inquiry of it am the said.
And the restoration be thus that enguales the fracture am the said according to the manner possible, until that it be engulhat according to that cove, and then plaster and strengthen the broken bone am stela, si indigeys of that.
And if the fracture of the back is declining to the parts of it, then occurs to the patient pain vehement and punctura, thus cum punctura that occurs to that one who has pleureci, and for that the bone puts the velament; and occurs to it difficulty of hanelit, and cough, and vomit of blood; and this is of difficult cure.
And already the Ancients so engeniatz in that with motz engientz.
And of their so that diso: cove that you place viandas of the patient that engendered imflacio and ventositat, for that the belly be inflated and be extendut, and the fracture be impellida to the parts of fora.
But we would not avoreys that for so that it not be cause leugiera to generate apostema caut, if it does not occur; and if it already occurs, then it helps ad that.
And diyssero alcus of them that it be placed desus ventoza and then that it is sucked am fortitut; and it is semblant to ratiocinacio, but empero it is temut that the ventoza atire the superfluitatz to the location for the debilitat.
And diyssero alcus of them: cove that the location be cubert am lana that already is submersa in oli caut, and that they be placed pulvils between the costas entro that it be umplida, that it be the liadura engual when it envelopes that according to retonditat; and then be curat the patient am curacio of pleuresi of vianda and of medicina.
And if vehement cause constrenh the patient which no sufferta, and the bone puts the velament am punctura ledent, temem sobre the patient, then cove that they fendam sobre the location, descubram of the costa trencada, and then placem desobre that instrument that conserve the ciphac, the rememoracio of which is precedida, and that they inscidam the os am facilitat, and extraguam that.
And then that they adjust the two labias of the plagua, if it is gran, am cozidura, and curem that am emplastres entro that it be sanada.
And if it occurs dedintz apostema, then you cut and enfon pulvils in oli rozat and place sobre the location, and that it be curat the patient am that which cove to the apostema dedintz.
And he should be laid on his side on which he sleeps, until he is healed.
Chapter IX.
On the restoration of fractures of the vertebrae of the back and neck.
Sometimes a fracture occurs in the bones of the neck, and it is necessary to treat them, and similarly for the vertebrae of the back. And if this happens to someone and you want to know if it will heal or not, consider this: if the muscles are already softened and stunned and dead, and they cannot move them or extend or strengthen them, and when you constrain them with your fingers or prick them with a needle, they feel no pain, then you know that it is not healing, and in the worst case, it is mortal.
And if they move and feel pain with constriction with the fingers and puncture, then you know that the bone's neck is saved and that the patient will recover with cure.
And if you see this in the vertebrae of the neck and want to know if it will heal or not, then consider his feet.
Then if you see that they are already softened, it happens to those who are affected in the muscles, and when you put your hand on his back, there is wind and expulsion of his will, and sometimes he is laid on his back and wants to urinate and cannot, then you know that it is mortal, and therefore do not work on his cure.
But if you do not see any cause of these things, the cause is better and less severe.
And the cure for him is that you perforate the abscess with a cautery and place it on the fractured vertebra with oiled, rubbed, or with the application of warm poultices; place that on the area several times a day until the abscess is cauterized.
And then place one of the comforting and existing plasters, and strengthen it on that with a bandage; and command the patient to use rest and tranquility and that he not sleep on the side with which he feels pain until he is healed.
And if it happens that the bone is shattered or any cause that is already separated from it, then cover it with a splint and support that bone; and then adjust the two lips of the wound, with sutures if it is large; and then care for it with some poultice of the entrails, until it is healed.
But if the tailbone is broken, and it is the end of the spine, then cover that with your left hand in the buttock, and align the broken bone with your other hand as much as possible according to the second equation; and then place a plaster and splint on it, if you need those, and then strengthen it.
And if you feel splinters broken in him, then cover that, and support it, and cure the wound with the cure that has been preceded until it is healed.
Chapter X.
On the restoration of the shoulder.
The bones of the shoulders are often broken, and if they break, the fracture of them is nothing but a communication at the extremities of them and are incised in the length and decline to the sides; and it happens to the patient pain in the place and puncture, and the limb is stunned when it is fractured.
And the restoration of it is that you extend your hand over it while it is over the fracture as is the figure of it.
And if the fracture is at the extremity of it, then you align that according to how it is apparelled by equation align until it is assembled to its natural figure.
But if the fracture is in the long or which already declines to the sides, then you make the patient lie on his belly until it is prepared for you the restoration of that fracture. And therefore when you have aligned it, place a plaster on it, and then place a splint of wood or of splint on it, and strengthen by stricture on which you do not fear puncture of fracture nor removal of the splint.
And align the concavity with that which fills it until that presses the stricture according to equality, and command the patient to sleep on his back or on his side.
But if an abscess occurs, then you cease from extension of it and its restoration until the abscess is cauterized, and place on it what has seated it according to what has been preceded; and then return to the restoration of it, and strengthen it as it is covered.
And if splinters occur or are comminuted, then you extract them.
Chapter XI.
On the restoration of the fracture of the arm.
The arm is what is between the shoulder and the hand.
And if it is broken, then the restoration of it is in one of two ways, of which one is that you take a stick of moderate thickness according to this form:
And tie two bandages at both extremities, and then suspend it from the elevated place.
And follow the patient on a chair; and then throw out his broken arm onto the stick until it reaches the tip of it in the middle place of which is annexed the curvature of the stick.
And then suspend on it some heavy cause, or the minister extends it to the sides; and then the doctor and equal the fracture with his hands together, until the fracture returns as it should.
And the other way is that the patient be laid back on his neck and suspend the hand of his neck with a ligature; and then command two ministers that one of them hold what is above the fracture with his hands; and the other hold the pus below him; and each of them extend to his part.
And if you want the extension to be more forceful, then you strengthen over the fracture with one ligature and over that with another ligature, and each of the two ministers extend to his two parts.
And therefore if the fracture is near the extremity of the muscle, then it is covered that half of the ligature be placed over the  citillic and the other below the fracture of the thigh.
And similarly if the fracture is near the thigh, then it is covered that the ligature be placed over that place and over that thigh.
Bind that with ease and work, and as soon as it is bound according to what is due and applied for good application, then strengthen that if it does not happen to abscess caut.
But if it happens to abscess caut, then loosen the stricture within the seventh day, and place upon it wool cloth soaked in wine vinegar and rose oil until it heals the abscess, and then you strengthen that.
And the way of strengthening that is that you place a plaster over the fracture, and then place a wrapping of new cloth over the plaster; and then adjust the arm on that support, and place your hand over his muscle.
And place the cloth and strengthen over the support the arm so that the arm be in place of stars, if nothing prohibits this from the broken bone.
And then use of stars.
And it is that you place over the fracture that star that be more wide and more strong of the other stars; and place between each star the width of the finger, and be the length of the stars according to the fracture with an addition of three fingers of all part.
And then strengthen over the stars with the stricture which I have said at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your stricture over the place of the fracture be more strait. And as much as is lengthened the fracture be the stricture less.
And therefore if you see the situation of the stars and the stricture as we have said in the hour in which you restore the limb to its situation, then you do.
And if you fear abscess caut, then you loosen the stricture and the stars within the seventh day as we have said.
And then consider the ligature in all parts for three days so that nothing happens in the place proximate or inflammation; or it is prohibited the food to come to the limb by disposition of superfluity of stricture; and therefore rectify all that according to what we have said.
But if you are upon confidence that nothing happens from any of this, then do not untie the ligature until after three days or more.
And be the sleep of the patient on his back, and his hands on his stomach.
And place over the support a cushion full of wool, and consider in all hour of the night or of the day that not be untied nor dissolved the figure of the broken limb, or that be the ligature softened, therefore rectify that all with your virtue.
And place the food of the patient according to the order which we have before said, in such a way that the food be subtle at the beginning until it is begun to strengthen; and then it is covered that you make the food of him thicker.
Because of custom of the support and of the bed of him it is that they be strengthened in forty days; and then it is covered that they be untied and be administered bath and be medicated with unguents those which rectify that.
And if the fracture is laid rubbed, then you do not untie from that the ligature and the stars within the days or within two months.
Chapter .xii.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called two radius; of which one is small, and that is the one that lies the thumb, and the other is large and that is the one that is placed below the small finger, from the side more toward the back.
And very often is broken the major radius alone, or the small one, and very often they are broken together.
And when is broken the small radius above, then the restoration of it is lighter, and the healing of it is lighter.
And when is broken the radius of it below the fracture of it is bad, and the healing of it is difficult.
And worse of that is when the two bones are broken together.
Therefore if the bone which is broken is the small radius above, then it is convenient to the physician, it is necessary the restoration of it, that place a small extension with ease until that be bound.
But if the large radius is that which is broken, then it is covered that place extension more vehement.
And if the two radii are together broken, then it is covered that you place the extraction more strong.
And it is covered that you place the figure of the hand in the restoration of it and the extension of it extended over a cushion, and be the wrist of the hand to the parts above, more out than all the other fingers, and the little finger be lower of the other fingers, and the patient be centered on a well-fallen chair.
And place a cushion in front of them in the height or upright position of them, so that the patient does not move or work.
Then the minister extends the arm of the party further back with his hand or bandage, and another minister extends it upwards seemingly.
Then the doctor aligns the bones as much as he reduces that according to the best figure that he can.
And if in the fracture of the bones there are splinters, then you must perforate to reduce all pieces in their place with your virtue.
And if there appears in it a splinter separated that pierces the tendon and it is not to your hope in the restoration of it, then you must cut over it and remove it according to the manner which we have said above.
But if the fracture is with a plaque, I have already made a singular account of this, and therefore take the cure of this.
And if an abscess occurs at the beginning of your restoration, then you line a cloth with cerot made of olive oil and white wax, and it is midway between thickness and subtlety, and strengthen the cloth over it with a subtle structure so that the abscess is yielded; and then remove the cerot and place an emplastre prepared of powder of barley with the white of an egg, and then place stars; and let the star be the one which is placed over that fracture more wide and more strong.
And know that the number of the stars of the arm are six in the major dispositons, or whether the fracture is in one focus or in two foci together.
And then place your structure over the place of the fracture most strong and most vehement, and when more it is applied to the parts above the fracture or to the parts below it, place the structure a little more soft according to how it is preceded the remembrance of it in the beginning of the chapter.
And the cloths that are enveloped over the fracture should be soft and moist cloths, and not hard or rough, and the thread with which it is strengthened should be of its own proper thickness between subtle and coarse, as we have recounted.
And consider the limb and the ligament after a few days. And if there occurs any cause for which rectification is necessary, as if the swelling which occurs to the limb, and then you must immerse the limb in warm water until that swelling ceases.
And let the limb not be constricted until it rests, and then return the structure.
And if the structure is already softened and the bone is already remade and seems like those, then rectify all of it with your virtue.
And consider more if the nourishment is prohibited to reach the limb by superfluity of structure, for then you must soften that a little and let it for a few days until it runs to that the nourishment, and then strengthen it.
But if the patient does not occur any of those causes that we have said, then he must not be untied for about 20 days or thereabouts.
And then let the arm of the patient be suspended by its own virtue of movement agitation, and make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in 30 days or 32 days, or at most it is restored in 28 days, and all of it is according to the disposition of the complexities and the dispositions of the virtue.
Chapter XIII.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the wrist and the fingers.
Know that at the wrist and the joints of the fingers, the fracture is rare, and it does not occur to them except for many blows.
And when a fracture or a sprain occurs to the hand, then the patient must follow the same as before, and place the hand of him over that extended one.
And then the minister extends the broken bones and the doctor aligns them until they are conjoined with good conjunction.
And then you must place an emplastre and cotton wool, if no abscess occurs; and then place stars above according to the quantity of the place, and you have already enveloped it in a cloth of it.
But if the fracture is in the parts of the fingers of the palm, and then make a semblance of a sphere of cloth, and command the patient to strengthen it with his fractured palm; then it should be strengthened with a long cloth.
And let the star be of leather with cloth in which it is elevated, so that the leather is applied to the palm; and strengthen it according to what you must.
But if the fracture is in the outer parts, then you must place stars above and another star from the underside in the palm, so that the hand is open and straight; and then place the structure, as if the hand is revolved to the wrist between the fingers with ligaments.
And if a fracture occurs to any of the joints of the fingers, then if the finger is put straight, it should be aligned as you must, and then it should be strengthened with the palm, and if you want, place a small straight star to it so that the fracture is elevated and not harmed.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, as in that of the middle location, or in the index, or in the ring finger, or in the little finger, then it should be aligned and tied with the finger that is adjacent to it; or they should all be tied according to continuity, and it is better; or place a small star on it, as we have said in the thumb.
And consider, in your restoration, and around that, the disposition of abscess cautery and oppose — in your operation — to what happens when any cause of those occurs, according to how the story of it is repeated.
Chapter XIV.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the bone of the thigh.
The bone of the thigh, when broken, is declared to the feeling according to how it is converted to the part in front and the most sovereign pus.
And the restoration of it is done in such a way that the ligament is tightened over the fracture and another ligament is left below the fracture, and the patient is prone on their belly and face.
And then the minister extends all the ligament to its part according to the quality, when the fracture is in the middle part of the bone.
But if it is proper to the root of the thigh, then the ligament is proper to the denolh to the part of the pentenilh, so that the extension is placed in its, and the other ligament below the fracture.
And similarly if the fracture is proper to the denolh, then the ligament is made proper to the denolh, and the extension is to the parts below.
And then the doctor aligns the bone with both of its hands until it is reduced according to the natural resemblance and the bone is joined by a good connection; then cover that with plaster and constriction, if no abscess occurs to the limb. But if an abscess occurs in it, then you leave that for some days until the abscess cautery is yielded, and then return to your cure.
The constriction of it is made thus: cover that over the fracture a hard bandage, and double that two or three times, and remove from it the superfluity; and then envelop the leg until it reaches the talo and the root of the thigh.
And introduce long felt between the thigh and the leg proper from the denol to the parts below, and the extremities of the felt come from the part most sovereign of the two parts, and then roll over the leg and thigh what superfluity of the bandage.
And then place over the thigh in the place of that fracture splints, and place from those a splint over the bone of the leg.
And then fill the vacancies between the leg and the thigh with soft cloths, so that the constriction is coagulated.
And then begin with strong constriction of the middle over the place of the fracture with three or four wrappings, and as much as you go away with the ligament from the place of the fracture, your constriction be smaller and more lighter and softer.
And then extend the two extremities of the felt to the parts below until it reaches the ankle of the foot, and tighten both extremities of the splints on the other part so that the ligament is not removed from its place.
And then leave the constriction on that, so that it does not occur to the limb corrosion or abscess cautery or inflammation and resemblances to those; and if any cause of those occurs, then cut yourself from it, and rectify what occurs all, according to what we have said many times.
But if in the bone is heavy of the bone which puts, then cover that according to what is possible to you; and if not, finish on it and remove that, and cure with what precedes the recollection until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the thigh is tightened another thing that are adjusted to it splints, as we have said in the adjutori and in the arm.
But however in the restoration of it in such a manner made, do not occur to the patient claudication; and if it is restored alone, another thing that is applied to the other leg, then it is necessary that he claudicates that of which it is always.
And know that the thigh is tightened in 40 days or a few months or a few less, and all that is according to the diversity of the complexities and of the other dispositions.
Chapter XV.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the kneecap of the thigh.
Know that fracture of the kneecap of the thigh is hard to endure, but it occurs to it many times attrition.
But if it occurs to it fracture, then it is not but fissure or crack in its parts, and that is with plague or ses plague, and over that all is established with the feeling.
And the restoration of it is in such a way that it is aligned what is broken of its parts with the ditz until it is aggregated and joined according to what is possible by equation and facility and fabrication.
And then place plaster and place over that round splint, if you indigeysses of that; and tighten above with constriction to what is convenient.
And then consider all the dispositions which we have recommended in the other fractures, as abscess cautery and semblance to those in such a way that it opposes to all accidents to what is convenient until it is healed.
Chapter XVI.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the leg.
Know that in the leg there are two bones of which one is thick, and it is named by the name of the tibia; and the other is subtle, and it is named fibula.
And consider the case of a fracture of this kind which occurs to the two bones of the arm, and by this, restoration of it is made as well as restoration of the arm equally, and the operation of it is one.
And if two bones are broken together, the leg is converted to all parts.
And if the most delicate bone is broken, the leg is converted to the parts in front.
And if the thickest bone is broken, that one is left behind, and it is of those causes that are not to your liking.
And then administer extension and equation and ligation of the splints according to that same alignment.
However, keep in mind, if the fracture of the leg is slight and has many stages, that the extension be less and lighter, and alleviate with your virtue.
And in the leg there is some cause for operation on the arm, and it is when you align the stars and that completes your whole operation, then you will take pieces of wood of pine, which is placed under the causes which are placed in soles, and are made from branches of palm; some of those which are of small thickness, and not of the hand, and the length of them according to the length of the leg, and from the bone to the very end.
And then envelope over each piece in two envelopes according to their length, and place one over the part of the leg on one side and the other on the other side, and be from the bone to the very lowest part of the foot.
And then tie the two splints in three places of two extremities and to the middle place, because with this structure the leg is prohibited from bending to the right and left part, and it stands for good station.
And now a splint of wood has been administered according to the length of the leg; and it is placed on it so that it preserves that movement.
And most importantly, that you do this in a fracture, and when with that it is properly called a plague.
And then consider every day the leg and study with it by further study, and you guard it from abscess and inflammation or other similar causes; and as any of those causes occurs, then oppose yourself to it with what is necessary so that it may be healed.
Chapter XVII.
Of the fracture of the talus and the bones of the foot and the like.
To the talus, fracture does not occur at all; but to the bones of the foot, fracture occurs some times; and to the toes of the foot, very little fracture occurs, and it does not occur to them except attrition in the middle of the disposition.
And if to the bones of the foot, fracture occurs and sometimes those bones be elevated above each other, then the patient places his foot on the ground prepared as if going.
And then place one foot upon that which is elevated of those bones, and then walk upon those, and press, and align those until they return to their places.
And then place plaster and stopa above, and place below the belly of the foot a small table to which there are two plastic caps.
And then strengthen that with the foot in a downward structure, because you have enveloped that with cloths and other causes of which you have indicated.
And as it passes three days or four, then you untie the ligament, because you will find the bones aligned, or broken or dislocated, and the operation in those is thus as you know.
But if some toes are broken, then restore those, and align them as I and recounted to you in the restoration of the toes of the hand. And then place on the broken toe a splint according to the length of the toe, and be a little wider than it; and below the foot, this table which I have recounted to you, and strengthen that with a decent structure.
But if that which is broken of the bones are two or three or more, then you place on each toe a splint of its quantity enveloped in lion cloth, and strengthen the table in the lowest part of the foot, and be standing on one of the caps of the table, the belly of the foot so that it has good retention.
And it is convenient for you that you do not forget that you do not consider all the causes which have been said to you in the other fractures of the accidents that we have said; and oppose yourself to every accident with what is necessary.
Chapter XVIII.
Of the fracture of the hip bone of the woman and the bone of the thigh and the groin of the man.
When the hip bone of the woman is broken, then make that cease in a cell lowered, and then incline that to the side of the back a little, and it be held behind.
And then the woman impales the hip with a cot, and it is made in the hip of her as if she hopes; and then the woman is concuted, and elevates the back of her little by little, because all that is thrown out until it is before the part of the hip as if she hopes and the broken bone returns.
And then place powders on the back of her, and when she wants to urinate, she removes the cot with ease and then urinates, and return that according to the manner according to which you perceived that at the beginning, and then return to her first powder.
And do so for seven days or thereabouts, for that is the restoration.
And if you wish, take a sheep bladder and strengthen over the wound a straw tube; and introduce the whole bladder into the wound, and then blow into the tube with force until the bladder is inflated within the wound, for the fracture returns.
And then fill it with wool and let it stay for several days according to what we have recommended, until it is healed.
And when the bones of the thigh of a man or a woman are broken, then administer in the restoration and stitching of it what we have recommended for the bone of the hip.
And what is right is not hidden in these proper fractures and that little is known, especially to him who has some study and use and understands this my book well; for that fracture signifies to you in the greatest dispositions the right way in the restoration and in the tightening of it, and therefore understand well.
But in the leg of a man when it is broken, take a bandage of wool, and put the leg in that; and then place a wrapping of cloth over it, and have it enveloped, and then leave it for three days or thereabouts, and it will be healed.
Chapter XIX.
Of the restoration of fractures of bones when it is accompanied by a plague.
It is convenient for him to whom a fracture occurs with plague, and especially if the bone is large as is the bone of the hip or the humerus, or similar to that, that you take care and flebotomize him as soon as the conditions of flebotomy to you or testify as we have said before.
And if the plague flows blood, then you must take care to the retention of it as much as you pulverize over it  zegi trissat, if to you another cause is not present.
And then begin to restore the fracture in that same day, and do not prolong it, if you do not encounter an abscess.
But if an abscess occurs, cauterize it, then you leave the restoration of it until the ninth day, until the abscess is subsided, and do not approach it on the third day and the fourth of all, for you will cause to happen bad accidents.
But if the broken bone is prominent over the uncovered joint, then you must take care to reduce it and align it with your hands with as much ease and as little extension as possible.
But if extension is not to your prepared and the alignment of it with your hands, then you reduce it with your hands with this instrument; and the instrument is made of iron, the length of which is of seven digits or eight and the width of which is according to the quantity of the plague; and for that it is necessary to the physician that he prepares of those three or four according to the quantity that you indicate to it in the cure in all species of fracture.
And let it be rounded, and let it have little thickness so that it not be bent of necessity the comprehension over it in the hour of the operation.
And let it be of sharp extremity at which let it be margin in its extremities; and let the underside of it be of tending thickness, and of the majority of it within the parts.
And this is the form of it:
And it is named in Greek “labre volentz atelarem”, so it is a little palette.
Because it must be placed the extremity of it attends elevated over the extremity of the bone prominent.
And impel it in a single stroke until the bone returns, and it is aligned with some alignment.
And therefore strive to align the extremities of the fracture between them, and if the extremity of it broken is subtle, and does not take that the instrument for good acceptance, then you must insert the extremity of that bone until the instrument has power of it.
But if you cannot reduce the bone of all with what we have recommended, then incise it with what you convenes to it of the incisors which we have said; or saw it with one of the saws in whatever manner it is to you possible.
And then scrape that which remains in the bone of asperities and of small splinters.
And as the bone is reduced, and then the reduction the patient suffers violent pain leading, then you know that the bone is not returned to its natural place; and therefore do what you confer to the patient great help.
As the restoration is completed which you make to the bone, and then immerse cloth in black stipt, and properly if it is in summer, and do not place over the plague cerot nor other cause in which there is oil, so that it not cause in it to happen putrefaction and corruption.
And then administer stelas in the hour of your completion of the restoration of the bone, and leave the plague uncovered as much as you incise the wound with forceps and uses of bandages according to the quantity of the plague.
And guard yourself with all caution that you do not strain the plague with the fracture, for many times have done this the foolish physicians, and have caused to happen over their patients death or illness corroding or ulcerating, so it is healed. And if your stricture is lighter, softer, different from the stricture of the other fractures.
And if the plague is large or the plague is bad, and you fear about that certain accidents evils the ones that we have recounted, and you feel pain in the place, then do not cover that with a plaster, but make a wrapping of hard cloths in that place instead of the plaster and tighten it with those.
And when after a day or two the plague begins to start, that in it there is generated a swelling, then you remove from above the cloth which you had placed on it with care, and then administer poultices, with those which of our custom is that we medicate the plagues, such as tetrafarmacon and similar ones to that.
And it is convenient for you that you untie the bandage and consider the plague every day morning and evening before it is consolidated and hardened, and that the limb be prepared in such a manner that the pus runs from it to the parts below with facilitated.
If to the plague pass many days, and it is not consolidated nor is the swelling separated from it, then you know that here are satagias of the small ones for which you must inquire the plague with a probe. And of those satagias those that are separated remove and extract, those that of them are not separated and put the limb and cause to happen pain, pierce you to incise those as to you it is possible.
And if it happens to the plague a suppuration or elution, it is stiomenios, or another kind of corruption and putrefaction, then you must put on each of those with that which is convenient to it of the cure of which is preceded narration in its chapter.
And of this in which you must know the best sermon and you make present your patient: when the bone is broken, support the limb as the bone of the hip and the auxiliary and similar ones to those of the large members, and that you do not presume traction of it, nor the extraction of it for many times happens by this the death; have left it until it can be healed, for very soon it will fall by itself after 20 days or 30. And then cure the plague if to you is seen, and if not leave that.
Chapter XX.
Of the cure of nodes that happen in the trace of any fracture.
Many times happens this node in the trace of healing of fracture, and mostly that it is near the joints, and is deformed by it the figure of the limb; and very soon prohibits the limb of its natural operation.
Therefore consider if the node is recent and administers in that medicines that have stipulated as such as aloen, and ensis, and mira, and sercacollo, and acrassia, and similar ones; in this way that you take from that some or all, and composes those with wine, or with album of hen, or with wine of vinegar, and put that on the node in linen, and constrict those on it by good constriction.
And leave the constriction that you do not untie that for many days; and then untie that and iterate another, until that the node is removed; or strengthen on that lamina of lead descentment, because lead has property of removing all that is inflated in the limbs.
But if the node is already in a worse converted and tight is and necessity provokes to remove that coydament, and then you cut that by the part pus sovereign of it, and incise the superfluity and eminent, or rat that with some rezzors until it is removed; and cure that until it is healed.
Chapter XXI.
Of the restoration of fracture when it is restored and then that the limb remains subtle and frail not according to its nature.
When fracture of bone is restored, and then that the limb remains subtle and frail, then that is not done but for many causes: of which the one is multitude of solution of the ligament and of the ligament of it made not according to that which should be; or for superfluity of constriction of the ligaments until that the nourishment be prohibited to run to the limb; or for multitude of superfluous embrocation; or for superfluous movement not in its hour; or for scarcity of blood in the body of the patient and for weakness of it.
And cure of that is to give to the patient to eat and to make the heart of him tender until that the blood be multiplied in him; and administer baths; and introduce gaug and letitia over him.
And then put a poultice on the limb, so that the poultice attracts to it much nourishment, or continue embrocation of it with aygua tebeza, until that the nourishment runs and returns to its natural figure.
Chapter XXII.
Of the cure of the broken bones when they are restored crookedly and prohibits the operation of them of that which should be.
When it happens to the limb already restored during the healing of it of crookedness and eminence of the broken bone and node, and it is for that feeded of form of the limb, but however the limb is not prohibited of its natural operation, then you must not receive the word of that one who exists that member be of new broken.
But already there have been many foolish physicians and restorers the ones who have done that in our land; and this operation is very vituperable, producing great fear, the sign of which is mortal.
But if the lameness and node are recent, then you must be embrocated with aygua tebeza in which are put mollifying herbs, such as the leaves of altea and roots of it, and melilot, and similar ones; and be made a plaster with a mollifying plaster such as dyallaquilon well made; or that is taken mucillage of root of altea and is contused with gray of gualina or with oil of cera, and be plastered with that; or that are taken figs fat and are trissadas with feathers of dove, and similar ones of the medicines that are called amermans consolidation.
And sometimes it is dissolved with continuous light friction with the hands, and movement of the limb is administered throughout the day.
But if the curvature has already been added and is made strong and converted into a worse condition, and the need for its cure with iron arises, then it is necessary that it is the most exposed part and the continuation of the bone is dissolved, and that which is superfluous of the knot of that part is cut with the bone with subtle scissors, and it is administered facility in that with diligence and study.
Chapter XXIII.
Of the speech in dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of any of the joints of its place, so that movement is prohibited, and the figure of the limb is distorted, and it brings upon the patient pains and great injuries.
Therefore, when an accident occurs to any dislocation, it is necessary to immediately try to reduce it, and not delay that reduction, because if it is delayed, the place is inflamed and very difficult to reduce the dislocation.
And for this reason, one should not be harmed or stretched during the time of inflammation, because many times it brings on spasms and sharp pains.
But when this happens, it is necessary that you get to the flaying of the patient, and then let it rest until the inflammation subsides; and then the limb is wrapped with warm water and oil, and it is reduced with facility.
And every member should be cured with that by which the memory will come back to its place.
And you have already ordered the chapters of dislocation according to what has preceded in fracture; from the part of the body most sovereign to the part most inferior.
Chapter XXIV.
Of the cure of dislocation of the lower jaw.
So the two jaws are dislocated if it is not tardy; and the dislocation of both is made in one of two ways: that they are pulled from their places by little movement, and are softened a little; or they are dislocated by entire dislocation and complicated, so that they are softened at the junction until the saliva of the patient flows and that it cannot hold it or close its mouth, and the tongue of it is enveloped in its word.
But when the dislocation of it is little, then in the major dispositions it returns by itself with little cause.
But if the dislocation is entire and complicated, then it is necessary that the reduction of it be administered lightly, and not be delayed at all.
And it is that the minister has the head of the patient, and the doctor inserts his thumb of one of his hands into the root of the dislocation of the lower jaw from the inside of his mouth, if the dislocation is of one part; or that he inserts his two thumbs together, if the dislocation is of two parts, and the other said of his hands are outside with which he equalizes; and he commands the patient to cease his jaws and let those go from each part; and the doctor equalizes the jaw and pushes the jaw until it returns to its place.
And if the reduction of it is difficult, and especially if both jaws are dislocated at the same time, then administer vaporization with warm water and oil, until the reduction of both is made lightly and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And therefore, when they return and are equalized, and the mouth of the patient is closed and is not softened, then put on those powders of cloth with cerot, and it is made of wax and oil rubbed; and then it is applied with facility with soft bandages.
And let the patient sleep on his back, and his head be standing between two pillows, so that it does not harm him to the right or left part; and do not let him chew for any cause; having his food be forbidden lion, until when the pains are reduced and the jaws are healed, then let him chew as he wants.
And this be administered with facility, and do not work on the opening of the mouth of the patient, the eating and drinking and spitting, until the jaw is healed and cured.
And if the reduction of two jaws when they are dislocated at any time and are not converted to their places, then many times this happens fever and continuous soda, and very much is alerted the belly of the patient, and very much and vomits pure bile.
And when you see this, then you know that it is pernicious; and many times that one dies who this happens to in ten days.
Chapter XXV.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the clavicle and of the extremity of the muscle.
The clavicle is not dislocated from the place from its continuation with the feet; and sometimes it is dislocated at the outside part, and this is declared to the feeling.
And the restoration of it is that the patient lies on his back and extends his arms. And then grasp the place with your palm with firm grasp, so that it will return.
And then put on that plaster and powders and strengthen that.
But the extremity of that which is joined and continued with it, is not dislocated in any day; then it is necessary that it be reduced and equalized according to what we have said and according to what is your equipment.
And then place a plaster, and powder, and bandage on it, and command the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until it is healed.
And with this same cure, the extremity of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter .xxvi.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that the muscle is not dislocated except in three ways, one of which is that it is dislocated from the part of the thigh to the part below.
And the second is that it is dislocated in the part below the muscle, and that is done quickly; and it is not dislocated in the parts behind by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated in the parts in front by the nerves.
And most commonly it is dislocated and thrown out to the parts below the thigh, and most in those whose flesh is little, it is easily dislocated; but in those in which there is much flesh, it is thrown out with difficulty.
And often accidents happen to some men by a blow or fall, and the muscle of them is separated from its place by a cauterized abscess, and it is cured by that which is already dislocated.
Therefore, know that you should reduce it while you are above it, and then proceed in the cure of it.
And it is known that the dislocation of it is when it is in the parts below the thigh; so that you may distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the healthy one, for you find between those a manifest diversity; and you find in the head of the muscle concavity and below the thigh the touch of the head of the muscle as if a gourd, and the patient cannot reduce his hand to his ear, nor move it by all movements.
If it is dislocated below the foot or above, and then you find a manifest touch, and it is not hidden.
And from this dislocation is made a light reduction when it is fresh and the patient is a child.
And the reduction of it is that the minister elevates his hand to the parts below; and then places his thumbs below the thigh of him, and elevates the joint with strength upwards to its place, and the minister elevates his hand, and extends that upwards; and then places that below where it returns slightly.
But if by what we have said it does not return, and it is from the beginning that the dislocation occurred, many days, then know that the patient should be bathed in lukewarm water and administered a poultice that softens and lubricates as if it were made of boiled roots of aloe, fennel, and melilot in water, and it should be administered.
And then the patient should be laid on his back and placed below the thigh of him, waiting for a bandage between elevation and constriction, and the physician places his hand on the sphere, and elevates the head of the muscle upwards, and pulls the hand of the patient so that it is not moved to the parts below, and another minister holds the head of the patient, so that it is not moved to the parts below, for that instantly returns to its place.
And if you wish, reduce it in this manner: and that is that you make a man present who is longer than the patient, and he is on the part of that side, and interlaces his muscle below the thigh of the patient and elevates his thigh to the parts above while the patient is suspended in the air, and another minister pulls the hand of the patient from the parts below his belly. But if the patient is light, then know that with him that other cause should be suspended, so that the heavy one is supported, for the dislocation returns instantly.
